==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Tunnelling is an exclusively quantum mechanical phenomenon where a system with total
energy E has non-zero probability to cross a potential energy barrier of maximum Vmax > E.
Atoms and molecules involved in chemical reactions are among such systems. This work provides
a theoretical overview of some semi-classical methods employed for the approximate calculation
of tunnelling probabilities in the Ô¨Āeld of chemical reactions. We Ô¨Ārst deÔ¨Āne some key concepts
which are helpful to understand the problem at hand, like the probability Ô¨āux, the potential
energy surface and the reaction path. A review of some prototypical ideal cases for which the
tunnelling probability can be derived analytically is presented. The semi-classical methods are
then introduced. The rest of the this MSc. thesis is dedicated to the application of the methods for
simple reactions by numerical implementation in MATLAB in order to assess their accuracy, with
respect to both exact known results ans experimentally available data. An effort of mathematical
rigour is carried out throughout the entire work.

Preface
This thesis is the result of the work done at the DTU Chemistry Department during the Ô¨Ānal
semester of the two-year long Advanced and Applied Chemistry Master programme I started in
late August 2015. With the supervision of Associate Professor Niels Engholm Henriksen, who
introduced me to the topic of tunnelling in chemical reactions, I have been looking at methods
which would allow to understand and quantify this effect.
Tunnelling is one of the most striking phenomena occurring in quantum mechanics, and very
many theories and mathematical tools have been developed in the past century in order to tackle
it. If the electron is the quantum system by excellence, whole atoms and molecules also have their
share of interesting quantum mechanical properties, tunnelling being one of these. On the other
hand, there is experimental evidence that this effect has an important impact on macroscopic
observables. Thus, I wanted to explore in more details how the tunnelling phenomenon arises
in the chemical world. In this work, I wanted to discuss extensively and rigorously some of the
methods that are used in quantum mechanics and which, sometimes, are not so clearly presented
in the literature. By doing so, I could greatly expand my knowledge in fundamental physical
chemistry and better grasp some of the fascinating phenomena of chemistry. Moreover, much
effort has been spent in implementing the studied methods numerically in MATLAB. The result is
a whole package of scripts and functions which, I hope, could be reused in the future.
I would like to thank my supervisor Niels Engholm Henriksen for his guidance and for the
fruitful and inspiring discussions we have had throughout the semester. I also thank Associate
Professor Ren√© Wugt Larsen for involving me in his successful campaign at the Synchrotron
SOLEIL that took place in mid July. I want to say thank you to all the people that I met this
semester in the department I had the pleasure to discuss with. I am very, very grateful to my
family, which has always been supporting me in my studies, despite the distance that has been
separating us in the last seven years since I moved from my home city, Milan.
To Cristina,
Bertrand and Fabio: Grazie, Merci.

Part I
Fundamental concepts

1
Tunnelling: a quantum mechanical
phenomenon
1.1
The tunnelling phenomenon
Consider a small ball, comparable to a point particle, rolling on the Ô¨āoor without friction so
that the net force it experiences is zero. Let its kinetic energy be T and its gravitational potential
energy V . At some point, it encounters a bump of height z. The height proÔ¨Āle of the bump deÔ¨Ānes
what we could call a potential energy barrier, since V increases uphill and decrease downhill
reaching its maximum value Vtop = mgz at the top. By conservation of the total mechanical
energy E = T + V , its value is the same at any position. In particular, this is true on the Ô¨āoor
and at the top of the bump, so that:
E = TÔ¨āoor + VÔ¨āoor = Ttop + Vtop
The problem want to solve is: for which value of the total energy will the ball go across the bump?
The answer is simply found by noting that the kinetic energy is always positive. In particular,
Ttop ‚Č•0 so:
E ‚Č•Vtop
is the solution. In other words, the ball will surely reach and travel beyond the bump if and only
if its total energy is higher that the potential energy barrier. If it fails to be so, the ball will reach
zero speed at some point uphill and reverse its travel direction to go towards where it came from.
For a given value of E, we can clearly and exclusively answer ‚Äėyes‚Äô or ‚Äėno‚Äô to whether the ball will
travel from one side to the other of the bump. This is obvious from common understanding based
on classical mechanics.
Instead, according to the laws of quantum mechanics, this answer for system with total energy
E below the maximum value of potential energy barrier is actually ‚Äėyes‚Äô and ‚Äėno‚Äô. There is a non-
zero chance that, when E < Vtop, one can Ô¨Ānd the particle beyond the barrier, even though
this is forbidden by classical mechanics. This phenomenon, called quantum tunnelling, occurs
exclusively in quantum mechanics.
Reciprocally, there exists also the opposite phenomenon,
called quantum reÔ¨āection, where a particle with E > Vtop expected to be found beyond the barrier
might be instead seen travelling backwards after it has encountered it. This probabilistic aspect
of whether the studied system can be found at either side of the barrier leads to the deÔ¨Ānition of
tunnelling probability or permeability [1] and, accordingly, reÔ¨āection probability.
The theory of tunnelling has been Ô¨Ārst studied in nuclear physics, in particular with the pi-
oneering works of Gamow on őĪ-radioactivity [2]. It Ô¨Ānds interests in electronics and chemical
3
==PAGE:16==
Tunnelling: a quantum mechanical phenomenon
reaction kinetics [1, 3] explaining the non-Arrhenius character of rate constants at low tem-
perature. Recently, quantum tunnelling in chemical reactions has been seen as an exploitable
phenomenon in selective chemical synthesis, where one can choose to work under ‚Äútunnelling
control‚ÄĚ, in contrast with the more conventional ‚Äúthermodynamic control‚ÄĚ and ‚Äúkinetic control‚ÄĚ,
to synthesise a target compound [4‚Äď7].
In the present work, we are going to look at methods of studying the tunnelling phenomenon
in the framework of chemical reaction. In particular, some of the mathematical tools that are
used in quantifying the magnitude of this effect are detailed. These fall into a category of so-
called semi-classical methods which are fundamentally based on the laws of quantum mechanics,
but introduce some approximations inspired by the laws of classical mechanics and that target
some aspects of a system that one assumes can be considered as classical [3].
1.2
Fundamentals of quantum mechanics
1.2.1
The Schr√∂dinger equation
In classical physics, the time evolution of a system can be fully determined by Newton‚Äôs second
law. In its simplest form, it can be represented as point particle and its position and momentum
can be known simultaneously at any time by solving Newton‚Äôs equations. In an actual experiment,
the precision at which one can measure them is limited by the resolution of the instrument that
is used.
In quantum mechanics (for more extensive treatises, see e.g. [8‚Äď13]), a system is not repres-
ented as a point particle but rather as a complex-valued function ő®(x, t) ‚Äď commonly known as
wavefunction ‚Äď of time t ‚ąąR and position1 x ‚ąąD ‚äāR which is a solution to the time-dependent
Schr√∂dinger equation:

Equation (1.6a) is called the time-independent Schr√∂dinger equation. It is an eigenvalue problem
where one has to determine all the possible pairs (E, Ōą). We call spectrum of ňÜH the set Sp( ňÜH)

The problem of determining the tunnelling probability is often addressed by considering a
constant stream of particles, with constant energy E, approaching the barrier from the left-hand
side [1].
This picture is suggested by looking at stationary solutions of the time-dependent
Schr√∂dinger equation, since the probability Ô¨āux is constant over time for such solutions (see
section 1.3.3), and by setting the amplitude B+ of the stationary wave ‚Äúcoming from the right‚ÄĚ to
zero. Then one can examine in which proportions the stream ‚Äúcoming from the left‚ÄĚ is separated
into two others, namely a transmitted one and a reÔ¨āected one, upon collision with the barrier.
The former Ô¨āows beyond it to the right, the latter goes back to the left.
On the one hand, this approach may be misleading, since no actual propagation occurs in sta-
tionary states. However, this directionality is suggested by the sign of the momentum associated
with each plane wave.
The answer to why one is allowed to write B+ = 0 lays in what happens in the time evolution
of a wavepacket propagating from ‚ąí‚ąětowards increasing values of x. Suppose that we prepare
a particle in a Gaussian wavepacket state with positive initial momentum average ‚ĄŹk0:

2

‚ąÄk < 0,
|ő¶(k, 0)| ‚Čą0
(1.41)
so that the wavepacket is essentially constituted of plane waves with positive momentum. After
the collision with the barrier happening around t = tcoll, the wavepacket splits into a transmitted
and a reÔ¨āected smaller one:

with positive momentum.

The Ô¨Ārst and second terms ňÜTe and ňÜTn in the right-hand side of eq. (2.2) are the kinetic energy
operators of the electrons and the nuclei, respectively. The others are potential energy terms:
electron‚Äďelectron repulsions ( ňÜVe|e), electron‚Äďnucleus attractions ( ňÜVe|n), and nucleus‚Äďnucleus re-
pulsions ( ňÜVn|n). Being a many-body system, the solution to the problem (2.1) cannot be found
analytically. Nonetheless, some reasonable assumptions allow to write |ő®‚ü©in a form which can
bring one closer to an approximate solution, yet exploitable and fairly accurate. This standard
procedure that we shall describe is known as the Born-Oppenheimer approximation.
First, by noting that nuclei are much heavier than electrons, it is sensible to assume that the
positions of the former changes much slower than the ones of the latter. Quantum mechanically,
this adiabatic process [11] can be stated as follows:

Equation (2.13) is the equation that governs the time evolution of the state of the nuclei.
Thereafter, we will call it the nuclear time-dependent Schr√∂dinger equation. The electronic energy
can be seen as a potential experienced by the nuclei dependent on their positions in space.
As mentioned above, a given conÔ¨Āguration needs to be speciÔ¨Āed by the 3N coordinates of
the nuclei. However, only the relative positions with respect to each other have an effect on
the electronic and nuclear interactions. Indeed, if all atoms are translated together along any
of the 3 directions of space or are rotated1 as a whole about any of 3 (non-collinear) axis, the
relative positions remain the same, thus leaving ňÜHe unchanged. One can indeed verify that the
distances ‚ą•RőĹ ‚ąíri‚ą•, ‚ą•ri ‚ąírj‚ą•and ‚ą•RőĹ1 ‚ąíRőĹ2‚ą•in eq. (2.2) do not change when applying the
transformations, ‚ąÄ(őĹ, i) ‚ąąJ1, NK √ó J1, NeK:
(RőĹ, ri) 7‚Üí(RőĹ + v, ri + v)
1Intended as simple kinematic, geometric transformation without dynamical effect (such as, e.g, centrifugal forces
causing distortion of the molecule).

(RőĹ, ri) 7‚Üí(MRőĹ, Mri)

matrix2.
As a consequence, only 3N ‚ąí6 combinations of the coordinates RőĹ,őĪ (3N ‚ąí5 for a linear
molecule, as any rotation about the axis along which the atoms are aligned leaves it unchanged)
are sufÔ¨Ācient to evaluate the electronic energy E. These combinations are referred as the internal
or vibrational degrees of freedom. The remaining 6 (or 5 for linear molecules) combinations are
called external degrees of freedom which can be categorised in 3 translational degrees of freedom
(which are usually the coordinates of the centre of mass) and 3 or 2 rotational degrees of freedom
(e.g. angles about non-collinear axes).
Rotational and vibrational degrees of freedom are generally not independent from each other,
as rotations give rise to centrifugal effects pulling the atoms away from the rotation axis, thus
changing their relative positions. Conversely, internal vibrations do change the moments of in-
ertia, affecting the way the system rotates.
This coupling makes the kinetic energy term of
eq. (2.13) somewhat complex. Nevertheless, this problem is not addressed in this work, as it
is not central in the understanding of the tunnelling phenomenon in chemical reactions. Thus,
we will not consider rotations of the whole system at all (except is Transition State Theory, sec-
tion 6.3). We will limit ourselves to mention that the separation of the rotational and vibrational
motions may be accepted under so-called Eckart‚ÄďSayvetz conditions [17‚Äď19].

The key concept that will be used in the following is the Potential Energy Surface (PES). Its
geometrical deÔ¨Ānition is:
S = { (őĺ, z) ‚ąąKint √ó R | z = V (őĺ) }
(2.22)
that is to say a hypersurface in the space Kint√óR. As soon as the latter is a subset of Rn with n > 3
(namely, a point in that space is given by at least four coordinates) it is hard to represent such
an object. Nevertheless, for diatomic molecules or, for larger systems, by Ô¨Āxing some degrees of
freedom, it is possible to have a representation.
2.1.2
Illustrations of the concept of PES
To illustrate the preceding concepts, consider a diatomic molecule A‚ÄďB in its electronic
ground state. The number of atoms is obviously N = 2. Hence, 3N = 6 coordinates deÔ¨Āne
a conÔ¨Āguration, but only 3N ‚ąí5 = 1 (linear molecule) degree of freedom has an effect on their
interaction. Intuitively, the latter is the distance r between each atom.
As the electronic interaction potential energy V is a function of only one parameter r, the
curve C =

(r, v) ‚ąąR‚ąó
+ √ó R | v = V (r)
	
is a one-dimensional line in the plane. Such a curve is
called Potential Energy Curve (PEC) and, for diatomic molecules, typically looks like the blue line
shown in Ô¨Āg. 2.1. It is the simplest kind of potential energy surface (and by being a line, it is
logical to call it ‚Äėcurve‚Äô rather than ‚Äėsurface‚Äô).
For bigger non-linear molecules containing N ‚Č•3 atoms, the potential energy is a func-
tion of n = 3N ‚ąí6 ‚Č•3 internal coordinates (n ‚Č•4 for a linear molecule), so the potential
energy (hyper)surface is contained in a (n + 1)-dimensional space, which obviously cannot be
represented on paper in a conventional coordinate system. Nevertheless, valuable information
can be obtained by looking at how the potential varies with respect to a subset of degrees of
freedom by freezing some others. For example, one can look at the potential energy of a tri-
atomic molecule A‚ÄďB‚ÄďC (e.g.
CO2) as a function of the distances RAB and RBC by keep-
ing the angle ‚ą†(ABC) = ŌÄ (so that A, B and C are aligned). The potential energy surface
S =

(RAB, RBC, v) ‚ąąR‚ąó
+ √ó R‚ąó
+ √ó R | v = V (RAB, RBC)
	
will then qualitatively look like the
one shown in Ô¨Āg. 2.2.
In these two examples, the potential has a (global) minimum. The conÔ¨Āguration őĺ mapped
to that minimum by V corresponds to the most stable geometry (or equilibrium conÔ¨Āguration) őĺ0
of the system, namely that minimises its total energy. It is characterised by:

. The minimum of that curve is the saddle point of the PES, whose
corresponding geometry (circle in (b)) is that of the activated complex. P divides reactants on one side of

where Sp(HV (őĺ0)) is the spectrum (the set of eigenvalues) of the Hessian of the potential V
evaluated at the equilibrium conÔ¨Āguration.
It is also possible to represent potential energy surfaces of reacting systems such as:
A‚ąíB + C ‚ąí‚ąí‚Üí[A¬∑¬∑¬∑B¬∑¬∑¬∑C]‚Ä° ‚ąí‚ąí‚ÜíA + B‚ąíC
where [A¬∑¬∑¬∑B¬∑¬∑¬∑C]‚Ä° is the activated complex of the reaction, namely an hypothetical, non-isolable
intermediate structure between the reactants and the products, as the A‚ÄďB bond breaks and the
B‚ÄďC bond forms. A qualitative form of the PES describing this system, by the same approach of
keeping the three atoms aligned, is shown in Ô¨Āg. 2.3. This PES features a particular point őĺ‚Ä° called
saddle point (circle in Ô¨Āg. 2.3(b)). It is a minimum if seen along the direction RAB = RBC, but it
is a maximum along the direction RAB = ‚ąíRBC. Mathematically, such a point is characterised
by:
‚ąáV (őĺ‚Ä°) = 0
‚ąÉőĽ‚Ä° ‚ąąSp(HV (őĺ‚Ä°))
such that
őĽ‚Ä° < 0
(2.24)
The eigenvector corresponding to the negative eigenvalue őĽ‚Ä° gives the direction along which
the potential is a maximum, meaning that it decreases on either side of the saddle point3.
Potential energy surfaces such as the one in Ô¨Āg. 2.3, when available, are very important for
the study of the corresponding reacting system.
It can be seen that it is indeed a potential
energy barrier over which the nuclei move. A successful reaction is one such that, over time, the
geometry manages to go from one side to the other, crossing the vertical plane/surface separating
the reactants and the products valleys. Classically, this happens if the reacting system has enough
kinetic energy to overcome the barrier height which is, at least, the energy of the activated
complex. However, as a quantum system, can also tunnel through that barrier, that is cross
the reactants/products separating plane even though its total energy is below the energy of the
activated complex. This is why it is highly desirable to know with precision the form of the
potential, as tunnelling probabilities are sensitive not only to the height of a barrier but, and
especially, to its width.

2.2
Minimum Energy Path and Intrinsic Reaction Coordinate
As previewed in section 2.1.2, a reaction path can be deÔ¨Āned as a succession of nuclear con-
Ô¨Āgurations going from reactants to products. Mathematically, all this sequence of conÔ¨Āgurations
can be represented by a one-dimensional smooth line ő† in a chosen conÔ¨Āguration space K. For
convenience we will work in the one in mass-scaled coordinates. The dashed line in Ô¨Āg. 2.3(b)
may be one such reaction path.
However, the sole deÔ¨Ānition of the line does not tell anything about how the system progresses
along it. It is also important to measure the distance between two points on the path. This
technical issue is solved by parametrising the curve, which consists in deÔ¨Āning a function ő≥ of a
real variable (say time) with values in K:

In the previous example, ő≥1 is
a natural parametrisation of the circle, whereas ő≥2 is not. For a natural parametrisation ő≥ of a
curve, its argument can be understood as a distance coordinate, justifying why it can conveniently
be written as a position x instead of a time t.
Thus, in the deÔ¨Ānition and parametrisation of a reaction path, we want it to associate the
notion of travelled distance with the notion of nuclear conÔ¨Āguration. A natural parametrisation
is then the ideal choice.
One special reaction path that can be chosen is the Minimum Energy Path (MEP), Ô¨Ārst in-
troduced by Fukui [21], that we call here ő†. It is the one that joins reactants and products
conÔ¨Āgurations through the activated complex‚Äô. The name ‚Äėminimum energy‚Äô is justiÔ¨Āed by the
fact that the potential energy of the activated complex is the lowest at the frontier between react-
ants and products, and no other transition states conÔ¨Āgurations mapped to a higher energy are
included in the path. Also known as steepest descent path, it is also the one such that, at each of
its points (except at activated complex), its tangent is collinear to the gradient of the potential,
indeed the vector pointing to the locally steepest direction.
By formal deÔ¨Ānition, the Minimum Energy Path ő† is parametrised by function ő≥ : R ‚Üíő† ‚äāK
such that, for a give potential energy V with a saddle point at Q‚Ä°:

write thereafter, when unambiguous and for simplifying the notation, V (x) in place of V (ő≥(x)).

the vibrational ground state is associated to the energy E0 = ‚ĄŹŌČ/2, also called vibrational zero-
point energy. This means that the energy of the molecule cannot be the energy at the minimum
of the potential V (r0), but rather the latter plus the zero-point energy, so E0 + V (r0). In the
classical picture, the molecule is then not ‚Äúfrozen‚ÄĚ in its equilibrium conÔ¨Āguration but is instead
constantly vibrating around it [23]. This is in agreement with Heisenberg uncertainty principle,
as the position cannot be known with absolute certainty.
2.3.2
Normal mode decomposition for a polyatomic molecule
The concept of harmonic approximation of the vibrational motion of a diatomic molecule can
be extended to others of arbitrary size. This procedure, known as normal mode decomposition,
consists in describing the overall vibrations of the molecule as a combination of more funda-
mental and independent collective ways the atoms can move, called normal modes. These modes
arise from a quadratic approximation of the electronic potential energy and each can conceptu-
ally be seen as one isolated harmonic oscillator with its own force constant.
Consider a molecule mode of N atoms. Its geometry and value of the electronic interaction
energy V can be deÔ¨Āned in terms of the mass-scaled coordinates Q ‚ąąKS as suggested in sec-
tion 2.1.3. The Taylor expansion of the potential up to the second order around the equilibrium
conÔ¨Āguration Q0, for a molecule whose vibration amplitude is small enough around it, is:

2.3.3
Normal mode decomposition for an activated complex
The structure of the activated complex Q‚Ä° of a N-atoms reactive system is mapped onto a
saddle point, that is to say a stationary point for which one of the eigenvalues of the Hessian
HV (Q‚Ä°) is negative.
At such a point, the same procedure described in the previous section
still applies, with the sole difference that the angular frequency corresponding to the negative
eigenvalue, call it ŌČ‚Ä°, is purely imaginary. As a consequence, the single normal mode Hamiltonian
ňÜH‚Ä° associated to ŌČ‚Ä° will not be the one of a harmonic oscillator but rather of a parabolic barrier.
Indeed, if we write ŌČ‚Ä° = iňúŌČ‚Ä° with ňúŌČ‚Ä° ‚ąąR+ and deÔ¨Āne the corresponding normal mode v‚Ä° and
displacement ő∂‚Ä°:

As a result, there are no bound states along that normal mode, meaning that the resolution eigen-
value problem ňÜH‚Ä° ‚ü®ő∂‚Ä°|ŌÜ‚Ä°‚ü©= E‚Ä° ‚ü®ő∂‚Ä°|ŌÜ‚Ä°‚ü©does not yield quantised energies but rather a continuous
spectrum (see section 3.2 for the detailed derivation). Thus, no vibrational zero-point energy is
associated to this motion. The total zero-point energy of the activated complex is then given by
the contribution of all the other modes.
Another important consequence arising from the normal mode analysis at the saddle point
is that the mode corresponding to the negative eigenvalue ŌČ2
‚Ä° equals, up to a sign, to the speed
vector of the parametrising function ő≥ of the minimum energy path deÔ¨Āned in eq. (2.32). One
can say, somewhat abusively, that the reaction coordinate along the MEP at the activated complex
is the imaginary frequency normal mode. For this reason, as will be seen later, one can model a
one-dimensional reaction potential barrier by using ňúŌČ‚Ä° as a control parameter which deÔ¨Ānes its
width. This is the case of the parabolic barrier (whose Hamiltonian would be exactly eq. (2.45))
or the Eckart barrier (see section 3.3.

We shall note that not having tunnelling as deÔ¨Āned in section 1.4 does not mean the system
cannot be found in the classically forbidden regions II or III. It is in fact the opposite: there, the
probability density is not identically zero and can still be signiÔ¨Ācantly high in the surroundings
of x = 0.
Such phenomenon is also seen, for instance, for a system in a harmonic oscillator or a rectan-
gular well potential of Ô¨Ānite depth. The eigenstates, in coordinate representation, are functions
which go to zero as x ‚Üí¬Ī‚ąě, without being identically zero beyond the turning points. That
allows then the system to be found at positions which are strictly forbidden in classical mechan-
ics. This possibility is also called "tunnelling", intended as the non-zero probability of Ô¨Ānding a
system in a point where it cannot be according to classical mechanics (see, for example, [23, pp.
327, 335]).

3.2
Parabolic barrier
A parabolic barrier is a potential of the form:
‚ąÄx ‚ąąR,
V (x) = V0 ‚ąí1
2kx2
(3.13)
that is, an inverse harmonic oscillator. This potential has a great importance in tunnelling theory.
While it is a simple approximation of any real potential barrier around the saddle point, the
time-independent Schr√∂dinger equation can be analytically solved in this case. The expression
for the tunnelling probability, valid for energies close to the one at the top of the barrier, can then
be derived. Notice that the potential energy of the system goes to ‚ąí‚ąěas x ‚Üí¬Ī‚ąě(hence the
kinetic energy becomes inÔ¨Ānite), conÔ¨Ārming that there are no real physical reasons to consider
such limit cases. On the other hand, as we will see, it is useful to see how the eigenstates behave
as we look at them at positions far enough from the maximum.
It is convenient to deÔ¨Āne the characteristic angular frequency ňúŌČ = iŌČ, with ŌČ =

such oscillator. Thus, the time-independent Schr√∂dinger equation reads:

Let us discuss the physical content of these asymptotic expansions (3.25) and (3.26). They
are essentially non-plane waves propagating with phases ¬ĪS(u) = ¬Ī
 u2/4 ‚ąíőĶ ln |u|

and with
decreasing amplitude of the order of 1/
p
|u| in both directions.
On the one hand, one could write S(u) = k(u)u, with k(u) = (u/4 ‚ąíőĶ ln |u| /u), so that
the imaginary exponentials can be seen as plane waves with almost linearly position-dependent
wave number. Hence, at points that are distant from the top of the barrier, the absolute value
of the wave vector is higher and higher as we look down on either side of it. This observation
agrees with what could be expected from a system in a negative parabolic potential in classical
mechanics: its speed will be higher and higher as it moves away from the (unstable) equilibrium
position.
On the other hand, the decrease of the amplitude to zero at inÔ¨Ānity implies that the probability
density will decrease as well. This reÔ¨āects the fact that it will be less and less likely to Ô¨Ānd the
system far from the maximum, as a consequence of the impulse getting higher as the potential
goes downhill. This rejoins the classical picture as well. For instance, the probability density of
a harmonic oscillator, at high quantum numbers, is higher at its turning points and lower at its
bottom. It is indeed more likely to ‚Äúcatch‚ÄĚ the oscillator when its speed is minimal (at the turning
point) than when it is travelling at its highest speed (by the bottom).
That said, we claim that in eq. (3.25) the term multiplied by őõ1 is the wave with negative
momentum, since for u > 0 and u large enough, ‚ąík(u) < 0. We set then the boundary condition
őõ1 = 0. Equations (3.25) and (3.26) become then:

Reduction to the parabolic barrier for low values of Vmax ‚ąíE
The tunnelling probability through the parabolic barrier given by eq. (3.29) reaches exactly
the value of 1/2 at E = Vmax. Approximately the same occurs for the Eckart barrier tunnelling
transmission probability, eq. (3.39), for chemical barrier sizes. This suggests that the former
expression could be a good approximation of the latter, in the limit of Vmax ‚ąíE approaching zero.
In the following, we show this result analytically for a symmetric Eckart barrier.
The reÔ¨āection probability is, when A = 0 :
R(E) =

system. Appendix A.7 gives an extensive account of such transformation. In particular:
Vmax = B

4.1.1

The WKB or JWKB (Jeffreys-Wentzel-Kramers-Brillouin) approximation is a method with
which one can Ô¨Ānd an approximate solution of the time independent Schr√∂dinger equation in
one dimension [9, 11].

4.1.4
Tunnelling in the WKB approximation
In the previous sections, we have assumed that p2 is everywhere positive, that is to say E >
V . However, there can be regions where the opposite is true, depending on the problem. The
positions where E = V , that is p = 0, are the so-called classical turning points, for they correspond
to the points where a classical particle, moving in one direction, reaches the highest possible
potential energy and moves back in the opposite direction. The regions where E < V , by not
being possibly reached by a classical system with energy E, are called classically forbidden regions.
Nevertheless, as we have seen from the study of analytically solvable cases (section 3), a
quantum system may still have a non-zero probability of being found within such regions, giving
rise to tunnelling. With the WKB approximation, one can address this phenomenon as well.
Let the classically forbidden region be the set If = {x ‚ąąR | E < V (x)}. Then p2 is negative
over If, which implies that p itself is a positive or negative pure imaginary function in that region.
Thus, eq. (4.7) can be written as:
‚ąÄx ‚ąąIf,
S0(x) = ¬Īi

(4.15)

‚ąÄx ‚ąąIf,

‚ąÄx ‚ąąIf,

As one could have already noticed, the wavefunction diverges when p(x)2 approaches zero,
that is at the turning points. It is therefore not possible to use its continuity to relate the expres-
sions before and after a turning point ‚Äď as we could for the ideal rectangular barrier. Nevertheless,
a solution has been found by giving a closer look at the behaviour of the potential at these points.
Linearisation of the potential at a turning point
Consider for the moment only the left-hand side of a given barrier, where x1 is the turning
point, the classical region is its left and the non-classical one is on the right. Now delimit the real
axis in three parts as in Ô¨Āg. 4.1):

barrier, so that the WKB forms on either side can be related to each other.

Simple model
Usually [9, 11, 26], the used expression of the transmission probability is not eq. (4.36) but

4.2. The extended WKB approximation: Miller and Good method

T(E) = e‚ąí2őł(E)
(4.38)
Using eq. (4.38) is the most practical way to calculate tunnelling probabilities. Nevertheless, it
fails to be accurate close to the top of the barrier, since then őł(E) = 0 and T(E) = 1. Unit
transmission at that energy is indeed expected in classical mechanics (a particle with energy at
least worth the maximal value of V will always overcome it), but not in quantum mechanics, as
no reÔ¨āection is taken into account in this case.
4.2
The extended WKB approximation: Miller and Good method
4.2.1
The method
The Miller and Good method [2, 27‚Äď30], or extended WKB method, uses some of the argument
used in the method described in the previous section (which we shall call thereafter ‚Äúconventional
WKB method‚ÄĚ). It is based on a different and more careful choice of ansatz for the wavefunction
to determine.
Consider the time-independent Schr√∂dinger equation of the problem we want to solve, Ōą
being desired solution:
Ōą‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(x) + k(x)2Ōą(x) = 0
(4.39)
with k(x)2 =
p(x)2
‚ĄŹ

potential energy. We call I+ = {x ‚ąąR | p(x)2 > 0} the classical region, I‚ąí= {x ‚ąąR | p(x)2 < 0}
the non-classical one and I0 the set of zeros of p ‚Äď that is, its turning points. Equation (4.39)
is generally hard or impossible to solve analytically. We then consider, in parallel, another time-
independent Schr√∂dinger equation whose potential W is arbitrarily chosen ‚Äď possibly similar to
V ‚Äď and such that an analytical solution is known:

The tunnelling probability T(E) can then be found by repeating this procedure around the
second turning point, then linking the asymptotic forms ‚Äúin the middle‚ÄĚ of the barrier, via the
asymptotic expansions. The requirement for this procedure to be accurate is that the two turning
points must be distant enough from each other, such that the use of these linked asymptotic forms
is valid. As a matter of fact, this is not always true: close to the top of the barrier Vmax, the turning
points are closer and closer. Moreover, tunnelling is predominant in the range of energies close
to Vmax, not only because of the low value of Vmax ‚ąíE, but especially because, there, the barrier
is much ‚Äúthinner‚ÄĚ. In the next section we will analyse a choice of corresponding Schr√∂dinger
equation that will lead to another, more accurate, expression of the permeability which takes this
problem into account.

4.3. Quantum reÔ¨āection and semi-classical methods
Another interesting approach is to see reÔ¨āection over the barrier as tunnelling in momentum
space.
This interpretation is suggested by Jaffe [26].
Classically, a particle with E > Vmax
has a positive momentum. It remains so at all time: it just decreases as it climbs the barrier,
then increases again while travelling beyond it. Quantum reÔ¨āection would be the classically
unexpectable event where the momentum suddenly changes sign, corresponding to motion in
the opposite direction. The author successfully derives an expression of the reÔ¨āection probability
when E > Vmax of the same form of eq. (4.38).
Another simple method of calculating tunnelling/reÔ¨āection probabilities is suggested by Gar-
rett and Truhlar [31], where the symmetry of the permeability of the parabolic barrier is used
(eq. (3.30)). The same symmetry is used in eq. (4.55) by still calculating the action integral in
the non-classical region. The deÔ¨Ānition is:
T(E) =

(4.56)
Equation (4.56) is the actual equation that we will be using in the applications in part III.

5
Tunnelling of propagating Gaussian
wavepackets
In this section we indicate a way to obtain tunnelling probabilities by propagation of wave-
packets over a symmetric1 potential energy barrier. The idea here is to prepare a free wavepacket
(x, t) 7‚Üíő®(x, t) far away from the barrier at t = 0 and see how it behaves after it reaches
the latter. In section 1.2.3, we said that a wavepacket can be seen as a superposition of plane
waves whose amplitude is weighted by its Fourier transform (k, t) 7‚Üíő¶(k, t). An intuitive idea
to evaluate tunnelling through the barrier then is, so to say, ‚Äúhow much‚ÄĚ of a plane wave with a
give momentum ‚ĄŹk belonging to the incoming wavepacket has gone beyond the barrier, keeping
travelling towards +‚ąě, and how much has been reÔ¨āected instead, going back towards ‚ąí‚ąě.
The most convenient form of wavepacket to choose is the Gaussian wavepacket. We will in
short introduce the deÔ¨Ānition and its properties in order to see why it is interesting. Then we will

travels towards the barrier, Ô¨Ārst as free particle, its composition in plane waves will not change
until it encounters the former. Thus, |ő¶(k, 0)|2 can be taken as a reference and |ő¶(k, t)|2 will then
change only under the effects of the barrier.

6
Objectives and method
6.1
Objectives
In the following, we will test all the semi-classical methods we have encountered so far in
order to see how good they are in calculating tunnelling probabilities. These method, we remind,
are:
‚Äď Simple conventional WKB method ‚ÄėsWKB‚Äô, where T(E) = exp (‚ąí2őł(E)) (eq. (4.38));
‚Äď Conventional WKB method with linearised potential ‚ÄėlWKB‚Äô, where T(E) = (exp (‚ąíőł(E)) /4 + exp (őł(E)))‚ąí2
(eq. (4.36));
‚Äď Extended WKB method (i.e. Miller and Good method) ‚ÄėeWKB‚Äô, where T(E) = (1 + exp (2őł(E)))‚ąí1
and the curve is symmetric about (Vmax, 1/2) (eq. (4.56)).
‚Äď Wavepacket propagation method (WP);
In a Ô¨Ārst section, we will give a look at their accuracy. Since, on the one hand, the Eckart
barrier can reasonably model ‚Äď with a proper choice of its parameters ‚Äď the potential energy
curve of a reaction along the minimum energy path and, on the other hand, an exact expression
of the tunnelling probability is available (eq. (3.39)), we will use it as a reference. By applying
all the methods on such a barrier, we will eventually compare the results they give with the exact
ones.
Then, we will apply them to real potential energy barriers obtained by ab initio methods.
From the calculated transmission probabilities, one can evaluate rate constant tunnelling correc-
tion factors. In fact, Transition State Theory (see section 6.3) allows to obtain rate constants
of reactions by using molecular partition functions from statistical mechanics. Even though the
basic TST takes into account quantum mechanical phenomena, such as the discrete energy levels
and vibrational zero-point energies, it still neglects quantum tunnelling (and reÔ¨āection) by con-
sidering the barrier classically (up to the zero-point energies). As a result, the calculated rate
constant is generally a lower bound of what the real rate constant should be, especially at low
temperature. The tunnelling correction factor is a term which is multiplied with the calculated
rate in order to give a better estimate.
The real systems which are going to be analysed are bimolecular reactions such as:
H + H2 ‚ąí‚ąí‚ÜíH2 + H
and
F + H2 ‚ąí‚ąí‚ÜíFH + H
71
==PAGE:84==
Objectives and method
We have chosen these systems for their simplicity and because they involve the transfer of a
hydrogen atom, the lightest element in the periodic table, which are mechanisms featuring tun-
nelling at a signiÔ¨Ācantly higher magnitude than in reactions involving heavier atoms.
6.2
Molecular structures and reaction paths obtained by ab
initio methods
The real potential energy barriers of the reactions to be studied are obtained with the com-
putational quantum chemistry package GAUSSIAN09 [32]. The following computations were
carried out:
‚Äď Geometry optimisation of reactants and products;
‚Äď Computation of their harmonic vibrational frequencies;
‚Äď Activated complex geometry search;
‚Äď Minimum energy path search and corresponding electronic energies (known as IRC calcu-
lation).
Preliminary calculations for all these were done with second-order M√łller-Plesset (MP2) per-
turbation theory [33] with augmented correlation consistent polarised triple-ő∂ basis set aug-cc-
pVTZ [34, 35] in order to obtain a Ô¨Ārst set of fairly acceptable, yet not totally accurate, quantities
and potential energy curves. The geometries obtained from this Ô¨Ārst round of optimisations were
subsequently reÔ¨Āned by further calculations at higher levels of theory. Coupled cluster theory
with single and double excitations (CCSD) [36] was used in combination with correlation con-
sistent polarised basis sets cc-pVXZ, with:
‚Äď for H + H2 ‚ÜíH2 + H:
‚Äď X = Q, 5, 6 for geometry optimisations, activated complex search and harmonic fre-
quencies computation;
‚Äď X = 5 for the minimum energy path searches;
‚Äď for F + H2 ‚ÜíFH + H:
‚Äď X = Q, 5 for geometry optimisations, activated complex search and harmonic frequen-
cies computation;
‚Äď X = Q for the minimum energy path searches.
The required convergence criteria for all computations were set to very tight, the strictest one
in this software. In the special case of the isolated hydrogen atom, the well known analytical
result of the ground state electronic energy (E0 = 1/2 Eh) is used.
The energies of the optimised structures, their harmonic frequencies and their vibrational
zero-point energies are summarised in table 6.1. The Ô¨Ātted1 potential energy curves obtained
by IRC calculations of H + H2 ‚ÜíH2 + H at the CCSD/cc-pV5Z level of theory and of F +
H2 ‚ÜíFH + H at the CCSD/cc-pVQZ level of theory the are shown in Ô¨Āgs. 6.1 and 6.2. For sake
of completeness, we have plotted in these Ô¨Āgures the corresponding Eckart barrier obtained from
parameters in tables 6.1 to 6.3 at the same level of theory. One can see that the Eckart barrier
of the reaction H + H2 ‚ÜíH2 + H is clearly thinner than the real one, hence overestimating
tunnelling from low to intermediate energies below its maximum. Due to the small barrier height
of F + H2 ‚ÜíFH + H, the real and the Eckart barrier of this reaction have comparable widths,
yet the latter overestimates the values of the potential energy on the products side.

Adaptation of the potential energy curves obtained by IRC calculations

certain j ‚ąąJ1, NK , xj = 0 and Vj = 0 corresponding to the saddle point. Unfortunately, the
pairs are not all equally spaced in the abscissa xi (this is necessary for the implementation of the
wavepacket propagation method, see below). Moreover, the program has difÔ¨Āculties in obtaining
the points at the bottom of the reaction barrier: since it becomes Ô¨āatter and Ô¨āatter far from the
activated complex, the convergence criterion is hardly satisÔ¨Āed. Thus, we need to be able to build
sets of potential energy curve data points that are suitable for our purposes.

Thus, we have a simple procedure to apply the split time-propagator to the wavefunction, for a

1. Multiply the wavefunction in coordinate space ő®(x, t) by exp
 ‚ąíi‚ąÜt

 ‚ąíi‚ąÜt

The result is ő®(x, t + ‚ąÜt).
In the framework of the computer implementation, wavefunctions and potentials are given
as vectors of discrete values. Fourier transforms in the discrete domain are done via the Discrete
Fourier Transform (see for example [38]), which is included by default among the MATLAB util-
ities. The vector of coordinates k is calculated based on the extent and the number of points of
the positions vector x.

==PAGE:99==
8
Application on real potential energy
barriers
The Ô¨Ānal purpose of the methods we have discussed in section 4 is to determine the per-
meability ‚Äď or tunnelling probability ‚Äď as a function of the total energy of a system, through any
arbitrary barrier. Here, they will be applied on ‚Äútrue‚ÄĚ potential energy barriers of the reactions
H + H2 ‚ÜíH2 + H and F + H2 ‚ÜíFH + H obtained by IRC calculations ‚Äď shown in Ô¨Āgs. 6.1 and 6.2.
Yet, experimental tunnelling probabilities themselves are not available, so there is no direct way
to compare our results with any reference. Instead, on the one hand, we are going to calculate
the rate constant of the reaction according to Transition State Theory and, on the other hand,
evaluate tunnelling correction factors with the calculated permeabilities. Thus, we will obtain
calculated rate constants that can be compared to experimental ones.
For the two reaction, we will present the results in a systematic way. We start by showing
the tunnelling probabilities curves calculated with the studied semi-classical methods. At the
same time, for the sake of completeness, we will also show the permeability curves of the corres-
ponding Eckart barriers using the same methods. Then we show the values of the rate constants
kTST(T) evaluated from data obtained with GAUSSIAN09 (tables 6.1 to 6.3) at the same level of
theory used for the IRC calculations ‚Äď CCSD/cc-pV5Z for H + H2 ‚ÜíH2 + H and CCSD/cc-pVQZ
for F + H2 ‚ÜíFH + H. We will call ktrans, krot, kvib and kelec the factors between parentheses (the
translational, rotational, vibrational and electronic contributions) in eq. (6.1). Finally, the tun-
nelling correction factors őļ(T) are evaluated and multiplied by kTST(T) to give the corrected rate
constants. Experimental values are then compared to our results.
87

A.7.2
Parabolic approximation around the optimum
We look now at the curvature of the Eckart barrier at its top. If we are to model the potential
energy curve of a reaction with an Eckart barrier, the parameter őļ can be related to the normal
mode of the activated complex along the reaction path by a parabolic approximation (see next
section). That needs to calculate the second derivative of V and evaluate its value at x‚ąó. Further-
more, this value turns out to be useful for the discussion on the similarity between the tunnelling
probabilities through an Eckart barrier and a parabolic one in the limit of small Vmax ‚ąíE (see
section 3.3).

A.7.3
Expression of the parameters in terms of barrier/well height and
curvature
Depending on the values of the parameter A (asymptotic value at +‚ąě), the Eckart barrier
can represent, in a rather simple way, the potential over which an endothermic or exothermic
reaction occurs. A, B and l also deÔ¨Āne its height and width. The latter deÔ¨Ānes the curvature of
the potential at its maximum (corresponding to the saddle point on the PES), hence the value
of the imaginary vibrational frequency of the normal mode, collinear to the reaction path, of the
activated complex. It is therefore useful, as done by [39], to express these coefÔ¨Ācient in terms of
more physically meaningful quantities, such as, on the one hand, differences between the energy
at the top of the barrier and those at inÔ¨Ānitely separated reactants/products and, on the other
hand, the imaginary frequency.
